[Bears]: from Mayor Bernalino Kern to fund General Stabilization Establish and Fund Capital Stabilization Funds.

[Bears]: Dear President Behrs and members of the City Council, I respectfully request and recommend that Your Honorable Body vote to appropriate free cash in the amount of $6 million into the General Stabilization Fund established earlier this year by Your Honorable Body.

[Bears]: As you know, the vast majority of cities and towns in the Commonwealth of Massachusetts have a General Stabilization Fund.

[Bears]: In order to provide for emergencies and unforeseen expenses,

[Bears]: a simple majority of the City Council is required to appropriate funds into a stabilization fund.

[Bears]: Further, in accordance with Chapter 40, Section 5b of the General Laws, I respect the request and recommend that Your Honorable Body take the following actions, each requiring a two-thirds majority vote of the City Council for establishment.

[Bears]: One, establish a capital stabilization fund and vote to approve $5 million of free cash to this fund.

[Bears]: Two, establish a water and sewer capital stabilization fund

[Bears]: and vote to appropriate $2 million of retained earnings to this fund.

[Bears]: As you are aware, free cash and retained earnings are only available after certification through June 30th of each year.

[Bears]: Each year without a stabilization fund, the city could face significant challenges in addressing emergencies or capital needs from July 1st until free cash is certified.

[Bears]: Chapter 40, section 5B of the general law stipulates that, quote, cities, towns, and districts may create one or more stabilization funds and appropriate any amount to the funds.

[Bears]: The Division of Local Services, Financial Management Resource Bureau,

[Bears]: Notes in a published article, quote, a city or town's capital assets are a vital part of providing essential services to residents.

[Bears]: Capital assets include public buildings and structures, roadways, parks and fields, large equipment like dump trucks, mowers, fire engines, fire breathing apparatus, police cruisers, radio equipment, as well as land acquisitions.

[Bears]: Major repairs to a capital asset or when it needs to be replaced unexpectedly or reaches its expected useful life early can be a significant shock to the budget.

[Bears]: Additionally, deferred maintenance of capital needs,

[Bears]: Existing capital assets is a major contributor to capital failures, as these necessary upkeep costs are sometimes avoided in the interest of balancing the budget.

[Bears]: One way to better prepare for capital expenditures, both planned and unexpected, is to establish a special purpose capital stabilization fund dedicated to funding regular required capital repairs and maintenance.

[Bears]: In close, please find a draft free cash plan for your review and input.

[Bears]: Thank you for your kind attention to this matter.

[Bears]: Respectfully submitted, Brianna Lingo-Kern, Mayor.

[Bears]: And in terms of the free cash plan, we have a free cash and retained earnings update.

[Bears]: Based on the unaudited balance sheet submitted, this is from the Department of Revenue Division of Local Services, notification of free cash approval.

[Bears]: This certification is in accordance with the provisions of Chapter 59, Section 23 as amended.

[Bears]: for a total of $14 million.

[Bears]: The capital stabilization funding mentioned would be a source for fiscal 2024 and prior year capital improvement needs.

[Bears]: 2.5 million fire department fleet replacements, 2 million pension liability, 2 million fiscal year 2025 capital improvements, 2 million fiscal year 2026 capital improvements, $9.5 million.

[Bears]: Please note that we do not anticipate future certifications to be as large as the last few years.

[Bears]: There are many factors that contribute to the balance in free cash today, including conservative estimating of revenues in pandemic years, vacancies in personnel positions, health insurance budget turnbacks, and the availability of ARPA funds.

[Bears]: The next one is, has there been discussions about an owner-occupied property tax exemption like Cambridge and Somerville have, and is that something the city council could pass?

[Bears]: So every year we use, we set the tax rate.

[Bears]: I won't go into the, take me more than 10 minutes to go into the complexities of exactly how Proposition 2 12 works, but we set the tax rate every year.

[Bears]: And one of the options that is, you know, we technically vote to consider is,

[Bears]: could we do an owner-occupied property tax exemption?

[Bears]: What that means is that the city would set a certain percentage of the average residential bill.

[Bears]: And if you were an owner-occupied unit, you would get that, essentially, your tax bill would go down by that amount.

[Bears]: Let's use some round numbers here.

[Bears]: Let's say it's $3,000.

[Bears]: that would be like a 30% exemption.

[Bears]: Let's say it's $3,000.

[Bears]: Now, I don't know off the top of my head, it's actually probably pretty close.

[Bears]: How that would work is everybody who pays a residential tax bill from, if your owner occupied, regardless of your value, you'd get $3,000 off your bill.

[Bears]: So if your house, essentially it creates a breakeven point where,

[Bears]: And it's a little more complicated than this, but basically everyone who's owner-occupied would get that cut off.

[Bears]: But that money doesn't just go away.

[Bears]: The city can't afford to actually have the taxes go down.

[Bears]: So that amount that owner-occupants get, that reduction, gets shifted onto people who don't get the owner-occupant exemption.

[Bears]: So essentially, you'd be shifting the city, the cost of the property tax on residential properties,

[Bears]: off of owner-occupants onto non-owner-occupants.

[Bears]: But the other side of that is that that actually changes the tax rate.

[Bears]: It essentially means instead of a flat residential tax rate on residential tax bills, you end up with a progressive rate.

[Bears]: I don't actually think that's a bad thing, but one of the reasons that previous councils prior to 2022

[Bears]: didn't pass it is it actually would raise taxes on owner occupants in high value homes.

[Bears]: So if your home is worth more than the breakeven point, even though you get the exemption, the tax rates going up more than the exemptions, you actually pay more.

[Bears]: The point being that it's a complicated conversation.

[Bears]: Essentially, if you are in a condo or a small single family and you're

[Bears]: living in that, you're an owner occupant, your taxes will probably go down.

[Bears]: If you're in an expensive single family or a multifamily, your taxes are probably going up.

[Bears]: If you're a certainly large apartment buildings, those there'd be significant increase because the rate would go up.

[Bears]: So it's not just this, you know, everyone who gets an if we implemented it tomorrow, that everyone would get this benefit in the same way.

[Bears]: It's a complicated conversation.

[Bears]: One of the reasons we haven't implemented it is because both the previous city assessor and the current city assessor say it would take them about a year to do the calculations and get everything in order.

[Bears]: And they'd probably need one to two more staff people in the assessing office just to process the applications for the owner occupancy exemption.

[Bears]: So it's a significant undertaking.

[Bears]: A lot of places like Everett's and Somerville's and Cambridge's did this a long time ago when the conversation was a little bit different because of the property values that we see today.

[Bears]: But yeah, so we've talked about it a lot.

[Bears]: I think I actually know too much about it because I don't know that I explained it particularly well right now.

[Bears]: But we've talked about it every year that we've set the tax rate since I've been on the council and I know on prior councils before that.

[Bears]: It would be a complicated undertaking, and it isn't the silver bullet that it's presented to be a lot of the time.

[Bears]: But do I think it's a bad idea?

[Bears]: No, and I'd be willing to move forward on it if folks felt we could make the time and effort and make it a priority personally.

[Bears]: 24475 offered by Councilor Scarpelli.

[Bears]: be it resolved that the Medford City Council hold a question and answer session to discuss the definitions and terminology regarding the proposed Proposition 2.5 overrides and the proposed debt exclusion measure.

[Bears]: To that resolution, the following amendments were added.

[Bears]: Councilor Scarpelli asked that the administration and Council on Aging do outreach to seniors about this meeting.

[Bears]: Councilor Callahan requested outreach to parents of school-aged children, not just teachers, and specifically requested outreach to the PTOs.

[Bears]: Councilor Sang requested general outreach and specifically requested that the community liaisons translate into other languages the information that is on the city website about the overrides and the debt exclusion.

[Bears]: To ensure that the council is able to answer questions in a timely manner and that decorum is maintained, the council president, myself, will be enforcing the following rules.

[Bears]: One, members of the public will have one minute to ask one question.

[Bears]: Members of the public will not be able to ask a second question until all who seek to ask a question have had the chance to ask their first question.

[Bears]: The president will verbally request that residents not ask duplicative or largely similar questions to questions that have already been answered.

[Bears]: and may move to the next question if a duplicative or similar question is asked.
